Preface
This project thesis has been prepared as a ﬁnal assignment to obtain the MSc degree in the ﬁeld of
Civil Engineering (Building structures). The work has been carried out at the Technical University of
Denmark in collaboration with Rambøll.
The thesis deals with study of the to examine the Deformation capacity of reinforced concrete
shear wall structures. Further various properties such as eﬀectiveness factor to determine the shear-
ing limit and the ductility index is observed. These observations has been compared with experimental
tests from the research studies and literature’s.
The work has been carried out at the Department of civil engineering under the supervision of
Professor Linh C. Hoang. I wish to express my sincere appreciation to my supervisor and guide for
his inspiring advice and optimistic encouragement.
I would like to thank from whole heart to my co-supervisor Kasper Paaske Larsen from Ram-
bøll. For the encouragement during this process and for his contribution with valuable knowledge,
encouragement and advise to nourish my interest and motivation.
I would also like to thank to Jesper Miller Andersen from Rambøll for providing diﬀerent tools
and making oﬃce space available throughout the process. Finally my parents Asha Soni and Rajesh
Kumar Soni and my wife Vismit Chawla for believing in my dream and supporting me through this
journey.
Rupesh Kumar Soni,
Student number.: S161002
Lyngby, Copenhagen, August 4, 2018

==PAGE:7==
1
SUMMARY
1
Summary
The Safety and designing of accurate modelling for a structure can be attained, if the performance of
a structure is inferred. The load deformation curve of shear wall structure gives a more convenient
perspective to deal with loading problem with eﬃcient and secure solution.
A parametric study on concrete specimen subjected to in-plane stresses is conducted. By consid-
ering the equilibrium conditions, compatibility conditions, constitutive relationship and stress ﬁeld
inclination these specimens were investigated under diﬀerent loading scenarios. Load deformation
curve derived from these studies and scenarios were compared from the test results of the panels
subjected to in-plane stresses.
Later on, Ductile properties in the panels is evaluated and compared with reinforcement degree
and loading ratio. These variation of the properties were plotted to see these varied pattern. General
formula of eﬀective factor were plotted along with the calculated response of panel to view shearing
in concrete. Constant k1 and k2 was established by co-relating the response and results values from
literature and parametric response. The factors k1 and k2 is then compared with the eurocode values
from EC draft. The calculation is compared with the test results, and a good agreement has been
found.

STRUCTURE OF THE THESIS
3
Structure of the thesis
The project thesis deals with the study of deformation capacity of reinforced concrete shear wall
structure. It is essential to evaluate how the structure deforms under various scenarios. Focusing on
this thought, diﬀerent consideration are prepared to sense the behavioural response of the panel. With
this understanding, an engineer will have a precise approach to the solution of the problem.
With this project research, a small element from a reinforced concrete shear wall is considered,
this small element is treated as an two dimension element which is called as panel and referred in this
report. The ﬁgure (1) shows a sketch of the panel from the shear wall and its approximate behaviour
under shear loads is approximately plotted, which is evaluated in the following sections.

panel
Various segments of report is summarized in the following section. The thesis mainly branched
into into four segments.
• In the ﬁrst segment of this report, main emphasis is given on literature review for modiﬁed
compression ﬁeld theory on the concrete panel subjected to in-plane stress and normal stress.
The concepts are indexed and prospects behind the inter-relations between stress, strain, load
and displacement are elaborated. Concrete softening eﬀect, eﬀectiveness factors with constants
k1 and k2, assumption behind stress ﬁeld inclination and ductility index and its variation with
mechanical degree of reinforcement is discussed.
• In the second segment of this report, the discussion revolves around the theoretical derivations
of panel behaviour under diﬀerent situations of loading is considered. These situations are pure
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 2 of 293
==PAGE:18==
3
STRUCTURE OF THE THESIS
shear condition, shear with biaxial tension conditions and ﬁnally shear with biaxial compression
condition, all these conditions are investigated for isotropic and orthotropic reinforcement. The
derivation further is considered to compare the experimental response from the previous litera-
ture’s. A Combined total of 69 samples are observed with the parametric studies. For pure shear
conditions 59 number of samples were observed. For shear with biaxial compression 6 numbers
of samples were observed, and for shear with biaxial tension conditions 4 numbers of samples
are examined.
• In the third segment of this report, the core discussion is based on comparison of experimental
values and calculated values from response.
The diﬀerent shear failure value is compared,
tabulated and plotted. A study on eﬀectiveness factors value is also conducted for constant k1
and k2 in this segment and results with its variation is also discussed. Furthermore the ductility
index is calculated and its variation with reinforcement degree is plotted and tabulated. Plots for
reinforcement stresses in the panel is plotted for diﬀerent condition. The variation of external
stresses with the B1 value is studied and plotted.
• In the fourth segment of this report, which targets on the conclusion of the study on properties
such as ductility index, stress variation of reinforcement, variation of concrete stress, factors
eﬀecting eﬀectiveness factor, and variation of stress inclination angle in developing crack phase
are concluded with future research perspective.

4
INTRODUCTION
4
Introduction
Over the decades, many studies were carried out to understand the behaviour of reinforced concrete
structure under various loading conditions. These loading can be static or dynamic depending upon
the source and nature of it. To understand these forces and adapting a system to counterbalance with
its stability is a challenging ﬁeld. The stability and behaviour of structure becomes more simpliﬁed,
if the response of the structure can be predicted. Predicted response of panel from a shear wall of a
reinforced concrete shear wall structure is a ﬁne line of research.
To uncover this response, material properties of its composition, along with its individual and com-
bined behaviours are considered and designed together to deﬁne the ﬁne tune response. All the material
composition bears property known as ductility. Some material have negligible ductile property like
concrete and some have abundant ductile in nature like reinforcement bar. Reinforced concrete panels
have concrete and steel as main composition, with the variation in amount of composition, the ultimate
failure and ductile nature of the panel shows deviation.
It is necessary to study and deﬁne an approach, which includes the failures of panel composition
at diﬀerent loading stages and strain values and its inter-relations. further these approach is elab-
orate to understand the inelastic deformation capacity of the panel in shear walls structures. This
deformation capacity is most important parameters for the resistance of structure imposed to forces.
4.1
Motivation towards current study
The behaviour of a panel loaded in uniaxial compression is well understood. Assuming that the panel
is adequately restrained against buckling, its behaviour can be predicted by a direct superposition of
the concrete and steel responses. This principle of superposition of stresses is applied and tested on
many samples over the years to examine the ultimate failure and failure pattern. It have been noticed
that, the failure of panel depends on many factors such as reinforcement degree, concrete strength,
material properties, loading conditions and many more. A failure in panel can be due to reinforcement
failure or concrete failure. The interest and motivation to understand these failure and deformation
capacity is the main objective of this thesis.
The study of deformation capacity is necessary to observe the behaviour of the entire structure.
Depending upon diﬀerent failures, a panel failure can be categories into three cases. These cases are
explained and shown in the ﬁgure (2).

The behavioural response of Case I and III, are generally seen in the isotropic condition, which
means both the reinforcement yields at the same time. Where as case II is an example of orthotropic
reinforcement, when the reinforcement in direction yields at diﬀerent instance of loading. These
response are possible for pure shear conditions, shear-tension or shear-compression case. In all the
case, initial steep slope shows tension in concrete, which is neglected in the model. The panel will show
its elongation properties until the ultimate strains value is reached by the ﬁrst yielded reinforcement.
These case are explain in details below.
• Case I : Failure before yielding: Considering the reinforcement in the panel is isotropic. As
the load increases gradually the concrete stresses tends to increase with principal strain. The
concrete in the panel reaches it shearing limit before yielding of reinforcement in any direction.
This is a case when the reinforcement degree are higher and concrete grade are weaker. The
response of panel is plotted in blue color, with a point A which shows the concrete shear failure
(crushing of concrete).
• Case II : Failure after ﬁrst yielding: Considering the reinforcement in the panel is orthotropic.
As the load increases gradually the concrete stresses tends to increase with principal strain. The
concrete in the panel reaches it shearing limit after yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement in weakest
direction. The response of panel is plotted in red color, with a point B which shows the concrete
shear failure (crushing of concrete). In this case concrete fails in shear after the yielding of the
weak reinforcement, upon further loading second direction reinforcement has also yielded.

5
FUNDAMENTAL & CONCEPTS
5
Fundamental & Concepts
5.1
Review of Literature’s
Study on reinforced concrete structures is considered to be one of the limitless ﬁeld for research and
development. This have been proved from many decades as results from many researches have opened
the doors to understand reinforced concrete structures.
In year 1979 Collins[18] has investigated the relationship between average principal compres-
sive strains and average principal compressive stresses in diagonally cracked reinforced concrete. It
has also shown that these relation are inﬂuenced by the magnitude of maximum co-existing shear strain.
The Compression ﬁeld theory was developed to formulated to model the behaviour of reinforced
concrete beams subjected to torsion by Collins and Mitchell [20]. Later on Vecchio and Collins
modiﬁed the theory to overcome the drawback and presented Modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory [3]
and [12]. They have presented as shear strain increase strength of the panel decrease and vice versa.
This MCFT theory was further applied to reinforced concrete beam subjected to shear in [19] and a
realistic prediction of ultimate load capacity and the entire load deformation was obtained.
Bhide and Collins in [13] also applied the theory to check the inﬂuence of axial tension on shear
capacity of reinforced concrete members. They have concluded that even members containing only
longitudinal reinforcement have considerable post-cracking shear capacity provided that the reinforce-
ment is capable of preventing excessive widening of the cracks.
Park, Sang-Yeol has used MCFT and ACI code in [22], to develop the load deformation curve for T
beam, with and without shear stirrups. they found that shear reinforcement contribution predicted by
MCFT was higher compare to ACI code, This was because of variation of stress ﬁeld inclination angle.
Belarbi and Hsu in [16] has simpliﬁed and presented the constitutive law of softened concrete in
biaxial loading and the diﬀerent parameter which aﬀects the peak stress softening coeﬃcients. In the
research they have shown the peak stress softening coeﬃcient as a function of principal tensile strain.
Vecchio in [23] has try developing the a Disturbed stress ﬁeld model for reinforced concrete to
counteract the limitations of inaccurate modelling of shear strength and stiﬀness, which was underes-
timated for panel containing high reinforcement in both the direction and shear strength and stiﬀness,
which was overestimated for uniaxially reinforced panels containing light reinforcement in transverse
direction (which is y direction as per our model). when modeling as per modiﬁed compression ﬁeld
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 7 of 293
==PAGE:23==
5
FUNDAMENTAL & CONCEPTS
5.2
Modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory (MCFT)
theory.
Zhang in [21] explained constitutive law of steel bars in concrete, it was discussed that, when
reinforcement is stiﬀened by the concrete, the post-cracking average stress strain relationship is
diﬀerent than a bare steel bars. Softened truss model was developed, and chalked down that rotating
angle model can predict the entire load deformation history. This was also discussed by Belarbi and
Hsu [14] and [15].
5.2
Modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory (MCFT)
Over the past three decades many research and theory have been developed to understand the behaviour
of reinforced concrete in diﬀerent conditions around the world. One of the popular theory also known
as the modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory (MCFT) which was developed out in the year 1986 by the
Professor Michael P. Collins and Frank J. Vecchio to observe the behaviour of reinforced concrete
under in-plane stress and normal stress.
This was demonstrated and proved by interpret the behaviour of a large number of panel loaded in
pure shear or in the combination of shear with axial stress. The experimental conclusion explains the
fundamental behaviour of the reinforced concrete in shear. These test were conducted at the university
of Toronto and later on same type of test were conducted by diﬀerent researchers and institute around
the world for precision.
Compression ﬁeld theory (CFT) which was developed to model the behaviour of cracked rein-
forced concrete beam subjected to torsion is the origin point for MCFT. In the study of CFT it was
assumed that in the cracked concrete the crack angle is rotating and the stresses and strains value is
considered average values which was aligned with the principal stresses and strains values. In this
model no tensile stresses was considered after cracking of concrete in the principal tensile direction.
Stress strain curve for plain concrete is considered for computing principal compressive strain, which
was one of the biggest drawback of theory, this is because the reduction in stiﬀness and strength is
not taken into account which was mainly due to the presence of large transverse tensile strain. To
counter-act this problem, MCFT was developed with modiﬁcation to provide compression softening
eﬀect (concrete in compression), tension softening eﬀect (concrete in tension) and a check for local
stress condition at crack location is considered. This alteration was necessary to establish an accurate
model to analysis the behavior in cracked reinforced concrete. With this fundamental concept many
researcher and institute incorporated this ﬁnding in diﬀerent ﬁnite element modelling and research to
get convenient to the actually response of the cracked concrete material.
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 8 of 293
==PAGE:24==
5
FUNDAMENTAL & CONCEPTS
5.3
Behaviour of reinforcement concrete panel
The modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory is a load-deformation behaviour model of two-dimensional
cracked reinforced concrete subjected to shear which give the insight on smeared rotating crack which
was observed in experiments. The concrete model is on the consideration that concrete stresses in
principal directions summed with reinforcing stresses assumed to be only axial. The most important
consideration is that the cracked concrete in reinforced concrete can be evaluated as a new material
with empirically deﬁned stress–strain behaviour. The behaviour can contradict with the conventional
stress-strain curve, Also the reason for choosing average strain and stresses is because the strain mea-
sured are piled together with crack slip, bond slip, local strain at crack and strain between the cracks.
for the stresses, it includes stresses at crack location, in between of cracks and dowel actions. For
the practices of these average stresses and strains to be a feasible consideration, the distances used in
determining the average behaviour must incorporate a few cracks.
Further on, By comparing the principal compressive stresses in the concrete with the corresponding
principal compressive strain, it was noted that the diagonally cracked concrete was not strong (weaker)
and softer than the same concrete tested in a standard cylinder experiment and that the diﬀerence
between the two behavioural responses increased as the magnitude of the co-existing principal tensile
strain is increased.
Also it was found that even after extensive cracking of the RC element, the Mohr’s circle of average
stress did not pass through the origin but showed a consistent and predictable principal tensile stress
in the concrete. Accounting for these tensile stresses in the cracked concrete changed the previously
developed compression ﬁeld theory to the modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory (MCFT) and allowed the
shear behaviour of both members with and without shear reinforcement to be predicted [3].
5.3
Behaviour of reinforcement concrete panel
The concept behind the panel behaviour, which was noticed and examined during diﬀerent experiments
is described in this section. When a a panel is loaded, it starts to anticipate with the load in the form
of deformation. The ﬁgure (3) reﬂects the behaviour of panel in the form of load deformation curve.

Figure 3: The ﬁgure illustrates the response of a panel compared between experimental response and
calculated response, and three diﬀerent region of failure
The complete response of a panel is explained in three regions with the important instance at point
A and B. The black plotted graph represents the actual response and colored graph represents the
calculated response from the model. Diﬀerent color in graph shows diﬀerent region, which are as
follows.
1. Region I or Curve OA: In this region the response is linear elastic region, the slope of the region
is at an angle. This angle denotes the stress ﬁeld inclination, this angle remains constant till
the yield of ﬁrst reinforcement in orthotropic panel or yielding of both the reinforcement for
isotropic panel. The experimental response shows a initial steep response in this region, this
is due to the presence of tensile strength in the concrete. This tensile strength is ignored in
the calculated response. The region will end at instance A, where the weakest reinforcement
has yielded. To evaluate and plot this region I, Concrete stress and shear stress at instance A
is computed with the equilibrium conditions, as stress ﬁeld inclination, reinforcement ratio and
which reinforcement has yielded are known. Now a gradual reduction in concrete stresses are
assumed, from these concrete stresses, corresponding concrete strains is computed. Strains in
reinforcement is computed from geometrical conditions. Stresses in both the reinforcement is
computed from the concrete stresses from equation (6) and (7). The corresponding strain are
computed from the geometrical conditions (13), further more principal strain and shear strains
are computed from equation (12) and (11). The response for these region are plotted from these
computed values.
2. Region II or Curve AB: In this region the response is no more linear elastic, the slope of the
region will continuously changes till the capacity of the strongest reinforcement attains its yield
value.
In other words, the stiﬀness of the material is reduced, and softening eﬀects comes
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 10 of 293
==PAGE:26==
5
FUNDAMENTAL & CONCEPTS
5.3
Behaviour of reinforcement concrete panel
in action.
To computed this region and plot the response, now the variation in stress ﬁeld
inclination is performed, so that the strongest reinforcement attains its yield value. With this
variation in stress ﬁeld inclination, concrete stresses and shear stresses are calculated. With these
concrete stress, concrete strains are computed. After strains in concrete, strains in reinforcement
is evaluated through compatibility conditions for both the reinforcement. With known values of
strains in concrete and reinforcement, principal strains and shear stains are calculated same as
region I.
3. Region III or Curve BC: In this panel response region, the panel is no more in the position of
carrying any further loading, as both the reinforcement has yield from the start of this region.
but the panel will still capable of carrying the same shear load, till the point B has reached. At
this location B the ﬁrst yield reinforcement has reached its ultimate strain value after yielding
from point A. The next important feature of this region is that, depending upon mechanical
reinforcement degree, ductility of the panel can be explored. This region of curve lies between
point A and B. This region is also varied depending upon the shearing limit of the panel. Which
reﬂects the ductility of the panel.
The graph shows the complete response of the panel under loading conditions. The stages with the
behaviour of diﬀerent reinforcement is plotted. It is much of importance that, to check the behaviour
of concrete which depends on the combination of reinforcement and concrete grade. Behaviour of
concrete and its failure pattern (i.e, crushing of concrete) can complete change the whole behavior of
the structure. The diﬀerent crushing pattern is explained in the graph (2).
Deﬁnition of the Problem
To investigate the behaviour of reinforced concrete shear wall structure. The problem is scaled down
to a small structural disk, which is examined and also referred as panel in this project thesis. A panel
is a structural element bounded by two parallel planes having a certain distance, and the thickness is
very small compared to the other dimension of the element. It is considered that the stress ﬁelds in
the panel is approximately a plane stress ﬁeld. when considering the loading of the panel, statically
equivalent forces to a plane force system in the middle plane of the disk. The ﬁgure (4) shows a panel,
with external stresses, shear loads and reinforcement ratio in x and y direction.

The reinforced mesh is placed in the panel with the yield strength of fy in two layers in x and y

x and y direction. The reinforcement area as per unit length of the bars in x and y direction is deﬁned
as Asx and Asy respectively. The reinforcement bars are rigid plastic component only accounted for
carrying axial tension and compression. The stresses in the reinforcement in x and y direction is
denoted as σsx and σsy respectively.
5.4
Equilibrium conditions
Equilibrium conditions - Stress and load relationship
The Equilibrium conditions deals with the stresses in the panel, which is induced in the concrete and
the reinforcement. The average internal stresses in concrete is given by σcx and σcy in x and y direction
respectively and the stress in concrete is given by σc. The reinforcement is given by σsx and σsy in x
and y direction with external stress is given as σx and σy. shear stresses is represented as τxy which
is acted on the edge surface of the element. The grade of concrete and steel is denoted as fc and fy,
with fyield,x and fyield,y as yield strength of the reinforcement in x and y directions.
Considering the superposition of concrete stresses and reinforcement stresses with an assumption
that the reinforcement bar can counter acts axial stresses only. The general equilibrium conditions
are represented in equations (5), (6), (7) and the ﬁgure (5) illustrates the stresses for concrete and
reinforcement.

5.5
Stress ﬁeld inclination (θ)
To determine the stress ﬁeld inclination the panel is considered to be in fully cracked state, which means
that concrete does not possess any tensile strength. The stress ﬁeld inclination makes the reinforcement
stress compatible with the longitudinal strain in the concrete [2]. Considering ﬁgure (6), the inclination
of stress ﬁeld θ can be determined by two methods, ﬁrstly by setting up equilibrium conditions in
the cracked state, this done when the principal directions of stresses and strains are coinciding with
each other. The equation to determine the stress ﬁeld inclination is given by the equation (8). It
is considered that the reinforcement forces are replaced by uniform equivalent stress distributions
over the concrete area. when initial cracking starts, and with the amount of reinforcement, the panel
undergoes crack developing phase, where the tensile concrete stresses are present in the cracked area,
with the help of bond stress the stresses is transferred from reinforcement to concrete.

zero, the reinforcement direction are the principal directions and the total stress is easily distributed
in concrete and reinforcement [2].
5.6
Compatibility / Geometrical conditions
Compatibility / Geometrical conditions: (Displacement and strain relationship)
One of the important consideration is the variation of strains in the material, which is referred as the
compatibility condition. The material responds in the form of deformation, when it is stressed by the
external factors. These deformation is should coincides with the deformation of concrete and steel.
Changes in the deformation in steel should in-turn matches with the corresponding deformation of
concrete.

Constitutive relationship

5.7
Constitutive relationship
Constitutive relationship - Stress-strain relationship
To make the simpliﬁed and the convenient model, it is considered that the average stress and average
strain relationship, for the concrete and the reinforcement is independent of each other, But in practical
sense it is not possible. This is mainly because of the bond present between the concrete and reinforce-
ment bars and also the layout of the bars inside the concrete panel. The constitutive relationships is
required to conceptualize both concrete and steel in terms of average stress and average strain values.
These average values of stresses and strain values may change from local stress and strain values when
tested as per standard test procedure in laboratory [3].
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 16 of 293
==PAGE:32==
5
FUNDAMENTAL & CONCEPTS
5.8
Softening phenomenon in concrete
The average stress and strain relationship consist of concrete in tension, reinforcement bars stiﬀened
by the concrete and soften concrete in compression. In the present model it is considered to use
simpliﬁed constitutive laws in which the tensile stresses in the concrete is ignored and the stress strain
curve of the reinforcement bar is assumed to exhibit the elastic perfectly plastic behaviour [14]. Since
the actual strain distribution along the bars are unknown, it will be considered that the average stress-
average strain relationship in the reinforcement which is given by the equation (14) and (15), where
σsx and σsy are average stresses in the reinforcement, εx and εy are average strains values, σyield,x
and σyield,y are yield stresses values of reinforcement in x and y direction and Es is the modulus of
elasticity.
σsx = Es · εx ≤σyield,x
(14)
σsy = Es · εy ≤σyield,y
(15)
The stress strain response of plain concrete diﬀer with high amount when compared with reinforced
concrete. furthermore it plain concrete cylinder cannot duplicate the strain conditions found in the
cracked reinforced concrete. Cracked reinforced concrete is often subjected to much higher tensile
strains in the direction normal to the compression ﬁeld [3]. So the fundamental consideration is
taken from the compression ﬁeld theory that is " The direction that is subjected to the largest average
compressive stress will coincide with the direction that is subjected to the largest average compressive
strain". It is assumed that the principal compressive stress and principal tensile stress can be co-related
to the average strain condition in the concrete [3]. The parabolic stress strain relationship deﬁned by
the following equations,

Softening phenomenon in concrete

laws of materials, strain compatibility and stress equilibrium. The softening phenomenon is eﬀected

5.9
Eﬀective factor (ν)
Concrete and steel reinforcement possess diﬀerent properties such as brittle behaviour of concrete and
ductile of steel reinforcement, which makes the reinforced concrete panel interesting when observed
the combined behaviour. Many research and studies have been carried out to simplify this concept.
Linh Cao Hoang has conducted a parametric study on eﬃciency factor, for diﬀerent cases in
optimum and non-optimum design which requires stress re-distributions in the form of rotation of
compressive struts. The formulation of eﬀective factor for diﬀerent case were present in his research
[25]. General formula for the eﬃciency factor is given by the equation (17).

5.10
Ductility Index (DI)
The ductility is the important property which is required for a structure to undergo large deformation
under a certain given external load. This properties is essential is earthquake resistant structure where
load reduction parameter is play an important role in reducing the forces by increasing deformation
in the structure. In the project thesis, the ductility index of a panel is calculated and compared with
mechanical reinforcement degree. which is presented in section (5.10).
The ductility of panel is deﬁned as the ratio of the total deformation at maximum load to the elastic limit
deformation. The ﬁgure (10) illustrates the basic concept of ductility index in a generalized response
for a behaviour in panel. In the ﬁgure ∆y is the elastic limit deformation (point of ﬁrst yielding),
∆max is the point of maximum deformation, which is either the crushing of concrete(shearing failure
in concrete) or point at which ﬁrst yielded reinforcement reaches its ultimate strain value. DIcrushed
means the panel has failed due to shearing in concrete.
DI means the panel has failed due to
reinforcement reached to ultimate strain value, shown in the ﬁgure (10).

PARAMETRIC APPROACH
6
Parametric Approach
This section deals with the parametric study and approach to derive the response of the panel. The
fundamental concepts for the behaviour of panel which was demonstrated in section (5.3) is broaden.
The calculated response for all the three region is presented in terms of shear load verse principal strain
or shear strain. Further on, a study of eﬀective factor is conducted to determine the constant k1 and k2
to match the results with the experimental values available from the various research literature’s. The
ductility property of panel is also studied and compared with mechanical reinforcement degree Φ.
Isotropic and orthotropic panels, are analysis under this concept for diﬀerent loading scenarios,
These scenarios are presented in three cases which are as follows;
1. Case I : Pure shear condition
2. Case II : Shear with proportional biaxial tension condition
3. Case III : Shear with proportional biaxial compression condition
6.1
Case I : Pure shear condition
The parametric approach for calculation is considered for unit length of the panel. The ﬁgure (11)
shows the panel with the stress under pure shear conditions. To analysis the panel, material properties
are considered, grade of concrete (fc) with Modulus of elasticity as (Ec), grade of reinforcement bars
(fy) with yield strength as (fyield), reinforcement ratio (ρ) and Modulus of elasticity of reinforcement
as (Es).

For Region III
The Region III, (i.e, Curve BC) as shown in the ﬁgure (3) is derived. Point B is the instance, where
both the direction reinforcement has yielded. The panel will respond till a certain point by showing the
elongation of reinforcement, but during this period no further load is carried by the panel. This region
can be deﬁned as the ductile region. The ductile region will continue till the ﬁrst yield reinforcement
reaches the value of ultimate strain. For this particular case, it will extended till the time εsy reached
5% of the strain.

6.2
Case II : Shear with biaxial tension condition
Region III
The Region III, (i.e, Curve BC) as shown in the ﬁgure (3) is derived. Point B is the instance,
where both the direction reinforcement has yielded. The panel will respond until a certain point by
showing the elongation of reinforcement, but during this period no further load is carried by the panel.
This region can be deﬁned as the ductile region. The ductile region will continue till the ﬁrst yield
reinforcement reaches the value of ultimate strain. For this particular case, it will extended till the
time εsy reached 5% of the strain.
Shear failure limit in Concrete
The shear failure limit is same, which is presented in section (6.1).

6.2.2
Shear Tension: Orthotropic reinforcement
In this part, orthotropic reinforcement is considered and material properties is tabulated in table (4)
along with the shear stress values calculated for the ﬁrst yielding and second yielding instance and
also reduction in inclination (∆θ) between ﬁrst yielding instance to the second yielding instance. The
calculation can be seen from the appendix (8.4). Depending upon the reinforcement ratio in each
panel, which leads to diﬀerent inclination of stress ﬁeld in each panel. This inclination will be followed
till the ﬁrst yielding is occurred, after this point a reduction in stiﬀness is noticed along with reduction
in inclination of stress ﬁeld. This will be measured till the second reinforcement attains its yield
strength.

Region III
The Region III, (i.e, Curve BC) as shown in the ﬁgure (3) is derived. Point B is the instance, where

elongation of reinforcement, but during this period no further load is carried by the panel. This region
can be deﬁned as the ductile region. The ductile region will continue till the ﬁrst yield reinforcement
reaches the value of ultimate strain. For this particular case, it will extended till the time εsy reached
5% of the strain.

6.3.2
Shear Compression: Orthotropic reinforcement
In this part, orthotropic reinforcement is considered and material properties is tabulated in table (6)
along with the shear stress values calculated for the ﬁrst yielding and second yielding instance and
also reduction in inclination (∆θ) between ﬁrst yielding instance to the second yielding instance. The
calculation can be seen from the appendix (8.4). Depending upon the reinforcement ratio in each
panel, which leads to diﬀerent inclination of stress ﬁeld in each panel. This inclination will be followed
till the ﬁrst yielding is occurred, after this point a reduction in stiﬀness is noticed along with reduction
in inclination of stress ﬁeld. This will be measured till the second reinforcement attains its yield
strength.

7
RESULT AND DISCUSSION
7
Result and Discussion
This part of report present the results of the parametric study.
While estimating and computing
the response, from the initial properties of the panel. These properties were assumed from the panel
properties to bring the results on comparable standards. During the process of computation, behaviour
inside the material due to its properties were discussed and plotted.
Firstly of all, Up on loading of panel, the variation in reinforcement stresses in both the directions
were observed at the same instance. This variation occurred when the external stresses are altered,
with a proportional loading in terms of shear loads. Secondly, The stress ﬁeld inclination is studied
and compared. Finally, the results were compared with experimental result to check the response and
the properties such as Ductility index and eﬀect of eﬀectiveness factor. The results from these sections
are tabulated and plotted.
7.1
Variation in B1 for external stresses
In this section a panel with isotropic reinforcement is examined and its various properties are listed
in tabulated in table (7). During this evaluation and observation, external stresses (σx) are varied by
changing external load factor B1 from zero to one in all the diﬀerent panels in a set calculation, but
external stresses (σy) is zero. The equilibrium condition to study this variation can be written as per
the equation (97).

7.2
Variation of reinforcement stresses
In this part of the report, four sets of isotropic reinforcement is considered. The panel containing these
reinforcement is stressed as mentioned in the section (7.1). The observation shows that, even though
the reinforcement is isotropic it shows a change and increase in reinforcement stresses as the loading
is increased due to variation in external stresses. The variation in external stress can be seen from the
table (9).
The ﬁgure (33) and (34) illustrates the reinforcement stresses (σsx) and (σsy) for panels A, B, C,
and D. In ﬁgure(33), shows the reinforcement stresses σsx and σsy for set A and B. For both the sets
A and B, ﬁrst σsx reaches the yield value and as the loading is increased σsy is gradually reaches
the yield value. The σsx and σsy values can be examined from the table (9) for the ﬁrst and second
yielding of reinforcement.
Similarly, Response for the set C and D can be viewed from ﬁgure (34) and stress value can be
noted from the table (9) for instance of ﬁrst and second yielding of reinforcement. In all the response
of set A to D shear stress value at ﬁrst instance is reducing, with increase in B1 value from zero to one.

In experimental data, it is found that variation starts at 27.65 deg, this is because of tensile stresses

but further the value of θ follows similar variation.

the reponse should continue till the ﬁrst yielded reinforcement reaches the ultimate strain value or
crushing of concrete occurs, (which ever points is ﬁrst).
7.4
Result comparison with experimental data
This part is focused on the comparison of calculated response with the experimental data of the panels.
Among all the 69 sample data, detailed calculation of 7 sample are presented are presented in the
report. which covers all the scenarios and loading cases. The samples are listed below.
1. Samples for Pure shear - PV11, PV22, PV26, and PV27.
2. Samples for Shear Tension - PV28.
3. Samples for Shear Compression - PV23, and PV25.
7.4.1
Comparison for pure shear condition
The detailed procedure to calculate response in pure shear, which was shown in section (6.1). This
procedure was implemented on panels from literature [3]. The panels which are presented here are
PV11, PV22, PV26, and PV27. And the response for each case is compared and presented. For
these mentioned samples, two graphs in terms of shear stress verse principal strain (τxy vs ε1) and
shear stress verse shear strains (τxy vs γxy) are plotted. In these plots, Variations of eﬀectiveness
factor is also plotted. The blue dotted line represents the variation for the drafted values of eurocode.
Simultaneously a study on constant is conducted and it is made to coincide with the results in case
where failure is noticed in the ﬁrst region (i.e, curve OA) and in the second region (i.e, curve AB)
marked in ﬁgure (3). The yielding of bars are also represented with the a colored dot on the response
curve. which was mentioned in each ﬁgure. Now all panels for pure shear conditions are presented.

strength in concrete, which is neglected in the model. Variation of eﬀectiveness factor and constants

Results comparison for panel PV27 for pure shear condition
The ﬁgure (42) and (43) shows shear load the comparison for panel PV27 with principal strains
and shear strains. As per the Panel response calculation, it shows that the panel will fail in ﬁrst
region before yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement. The panel have no ductile property, the ductility index
computed as 0.84. The eﬀectiveness factor from euro-codes also gives results, which is in the ﬁrst
region. Shearing of concrete can be seen before yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement. from [3], which states
that, At 5.24 MPa, ﬁrst signs of concrete crushing. At 6.21 MPa, concrete extensively damaged. At
6.35 MPa, brittle sliding shear failure parallel to longitudinal reinforcement.

7.4.2
Comparison for shear tension condition
The detailed procedure to calculate response in pure shear, which was shown in section (6.2). This
procedure was implemented on panels from literature [3]. The panels which are presented here are
PV28. And the response for the case is compared and presented. For the mentioned samples, two
graphs in terms of shear stress verse principal strain (τxy vs ε1) and shear stress verse shear strains (τxy
vs γxy) are plotted. In these plots, Variations of eﬀectiveness factor is also plotted. The blue dotted
line represents the variation for the drafted values of eurocode. Simultaneously a study on constant is
conducted and it is made to coincide with the results in case where failure is noticed in the ﬁrst region
(i.e, curve OA) and in the second region (i.e, curve AB) marked in ﬁgure (3). The yielding of bars are
also represented with the a colored dot on the response curve. which was mentioned in each ﬁgure.

7.4.3
Comparison for shear compression condition
The detailed procedure to calculate response in pure shear, which was shown in section (6.3). This
procedure was implemented on panels from literature [3]. The panels which are presented here are
PV23, and PV25. And the response for each case is compared and presented. For these mentioned
samples, two graphs in terms of shear stress verse principal strain (τxy vs ε1) and shear stress verse
shear strains (τxy vs γxy) are plotted. In these plots, Variations of eﬀectiveness factor is also plotted.

study on constant is conducted and it is made to coincide with the results in case where failure is
noticed in the ﬁrst region (i.e, curve OA) and in the second region (i.e, curve AB) marked in ﬁgure
(3). The yielding of bars are also represented with the a colored dot on the response curve. which was
mentioned in each ﬁgure.

The mean and standard deviation for all the sample evaluated gives a good decent results and are
presented in the table (16) which is 1.05 and 0.19. These results are tabulated and plotted in the ﬁgure

pure shear conditions are tabulated in table (15) and (16).
Notations used in all the case are highlighted below,
Crushed refers that, the concrete has sheared in ﬁrst region (i.e, curve OA)
First yield refers that, the failure after yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement (i.e, curve AB).
Both yield refers that, the failure of both the reinforcement (i.e, curve BC).
First second refers that, the failure, after ﬁrst reinforcement just before failure of second reinforcement
(i.e, Point B).

7.5
Ductility Index
The ﬁgures (54) and (55), shows the ductility index plotted against reinforcement degree for x and y
direction. From the ﬁgure it can be seen that the ductility index depends on the reinforcement degree,
yield strength and rate of loading. Sample PV27 from table shows higher value of reinforcement
degree and lesser the ductility index. PV2 and PV4 shows lesser reinforcement degree leads to higher
ductility index. for samples PHS2, PHS3, and PHS8 if the reinforcement degree of one of the direction
reinforcement is higher compare to another direction. These case also shows higher values of ductility
index.
Ductility Index (y-axis) vs Reinforcement degree-x direction (x-axis)
Figure 54: The ﬁgure shows comparison for properties ductility index and reinforcement degree in x
direction for Pure shear conditions
The ﬁgures (54) and (55), also shows that, when one direction reinforcement degree is weak but
other direction reinforcement degree is stronger, in this case also it leads to the higher ductility index.
Because the stronger reinforcement takes times to yield, which leads to higher performance in loading,
hence higher ductility.

8
CONCLUSION AND FUTURE STUDIES
8
Conclusion and Future studies
A parametric study to derive the response of a panel is presented in this project thesis. This project
thesis has utilized the concept of modiﬁed compression ﬁeld theory.
This theory have played a
signiﬁcant role in simplifying the behaviour of reinforced concrete shear wall structures subjected to
in-plane conditions by considering stress-strain relationship, equilibrium conditions and compatibility
conditions. The theory is used as an analytical tool to predict the response of the panel. Diﬀerent
loading conditions is considered such as pure shear, shear tension and shear compression, responses
under these conditions have been developed. The calculation procedure and sheets are present in
this report considering combined behaviour of concrete and reinforcement, and how the stresses are
related to each other.
8.1
Response of panels
The response of model depends on the constitute of the panel or structure. Depending upon the
reinforcement ratio, concrete and reinforcement properties the shear load capacity and ultimate failure
can be determined. It is noticed that, a variation in stress ﬁeld inclination is seen in orthotropic
reinforcement in between the region of ﬁrst and second yielding of reinforcement. In this sections the
stiﬀness of the panel is reduced, and the material should be treated as a new material with reduced
stiﬀness. The mean value for the ratio of experimental value to calculated response value for pure
shear is computed as 0.98, for shear tension as 1.02 and for for shear compression as 1.05.
8.2
Ductility index
Ductility is the properties required by a structure to respond against loading by exhibiting deformation.
with this studies it an be seen that, ductility index of a panel depends on the reinforcement degree,
loading rates which were present and seen in various graph in discussion. The value of ductility index
is less that 1 if the concrete in the panel is crushed before yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement. where as the
value of ductility index is more than one, if the panel is failed after the yielding of ﬁrst reinforcement.
8.3
Eﬀectiveness factor
Eﬀective factors deﬁnes the shearing limit of concrete, by considering softening eﬀect in the panel.
The constant mentions in the drafted for general formula for eﬃciency factor is observed and studied.
The draft suggest the value of k1 as 1.2 and k1 as 80. By studying response and comparing with
results, In here k1 as 1.0, and k2 is varied. for pure shear k2 as 131, for shear tension k2 as 117.5 and
for shear compression k2 is 70.
Dtu-Civil Engineering
Page 95 of 293
==PAGE:111==
8
CONCLUSION AND FUTURE STUDIES
8.4
Future studies
8.4
Future studies
The response and concept can be used, to compare the results from ﬁnite element results. Rambøll
has developed a numerical tool known as Nucleus to determine the ultimate load carrying capacity of
reinforced concrete structures based on a rigid-perfectly plastic material model. Due to lack of time,
comparison studies have not been done. These comparison study can be made as future research work.

Appendices
During the project work, a total 69 samples for various conditions were tested and compared. Which
makes this project report very long, because of this limitation, only test sample from [3] were
presented. This reference contains samples of pure shear, shear tension and shear compression. Other
ﬁles showing a set of calculation is a presented and can be seen in appendix.
1. Samples for Pure shear - PV11, PV22, PV26, and PV27.
2. Samples for Shear Tension - PV28.
3. Samples for Shear Compression - PV23, and PV25.
